رباية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that spiritual progress can be achieved by donating wealth alone.
Correction: Dardeer emphasizes that the true sacrifice is of one's self, not one's wealth; without giving your soul, even giving the whole world is considered stinginess in the love of Allah.

Checkpoint
What is the first step the author recommends for the seeker?
A) Finding a shaykh immediately.
B) Adhering to taqwa, resorting to Allah, and seeking intercession through the Prophet.
C) Giving away all wealth.
D) Isolating from all people.

Key Takeaway
The path to tawhid requires sincerity, taqwa, a qualified shaykh, complete submission, and the giving of one's entire self.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you truly willing to be like a corpse in the hands of a shaykh, or do you still cling to control over your own life, decisions, and plans? What would it take for you to let go completely?
?
Have you ever been generous with your self—your time, energy, comfort, and desires—in the path of Allah, or do you only give from your surplus wealth? What is one area of your life you have not yet surrendered?
?
If you are not under the guidance of a qualified shaykh, what is holding you back? Is it pride, fear of commitment, or a hidden belief that you can go it alone? Will you now sincerely pray for Allah to bring you to a guide, as the passage instructs?

The Spiritual Practice of Hunger and Its Effect on Deeds
Dardeer explains the spiritual practice of hunger and its connection to deed quality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the spiritual discipline of voluntary hunger, its connection to fasting, and how the quality of food influences the quality of one's deeds.

Core Idea
What you eat shapes what you do — even in spiritual practice.

Imam Dardeer presents hunger as a chosen practice, not a compulsory one. He defines its minimal form and explains that its difficulty is eased by frequent fasting. Then he draws a profound link: our actions are the fruit of what we consume. This lesson explores that link.

Guided Path
1
Understand hunger as a choice
Read the passage and note that hunger is described as 'ikhtiyar' (choice), meaning it is a voluntary discipline, not an obligation. Its minimum is one light meal per day from halal food.
Halal here is defined as food whose origin is unknown.
2
Recognize the role of fasting
Fasting is presented as the 'bridle' that helps travelers control their desires. See how fasting assists in adopting the practice of hunger.
3
Learn the food-deed connection
The author explicitly states that actions are the fruit of what is eaten. Identify the three categories of food (haram, halal, mutashabih) and their corresponding effects on deeds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس: الجوعُ اختيار، بأن لا يأكُلَ أكثَرَ مِنْ أَكْلِةٍ خفيفةٍ فِي يَومِه ولَيلَتِه مِنَ الحلال، وهو ما جهل أصله، ولا يمكنه ذلك في ابتداء أمره إلا بكثرة الصوم، فإنه لجام السائرين.
واعلم أنَّ العمل ثمرة المأكول، فالأكل الحرام لا ينشأ عنه إلا أعمال خبيثةٌ مُحَرَّمَةٌ، والحلال الصرف لا ينشأ عنه إلا الأعمال الصالحة، والمتشابه ينشأ عنه أعمال مختلطةٌ لا تخلو عن الرياء والعُجب والخواطر الرديئة.
The sixth: hunger is a choice (ikhtiyar), that one should not eat more than one light meal in his day and night from what is halal, that is, what one does not know its origin, and he cannot do that at the beginning of his affair except by much fasting, for it is the bridle of the travelers. And know that action is the fruit of what is eaten; forbidden food gives rise only to evil, forbidden deeds; pure halal gives rise only to righteous deeds; and ambiguous (mutashabih) food gives rise to mixed deeds that are not free from showing off, self-admiration, and evil thoughts.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the sixth spiritual practice: hunger (al-ju'). The author describes hunger as a chosen practice (ikhtiyar), not a compulsory one. Its minimal form is to eat no more than one light meal per day and night from halal food. 'Halal' here is defined as food whose origin is unknown (i.e., one is uncertain about its permissibility, so it is ruled as halal by default). At the beginning, this practice is difficult and requires much fasting. Fasting acts as a 'bridle' for the travelers on the path, restraining the lower self. The author then draws a direct causal link between food and actions: actions are the fruit of what one eats. Haram food produces only evil and prohibited deeds; pure halal produces only good deeds; and ambiguous (mutashabih) food produces mixed deeds tainted by showing off, self-admiration, and evil thoughts.

Key Terms
جوع
اختيار
حلال
حرام
متشابه
صوم
لجام السائرين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think hunger is merely about reducing food, ignoring the quality of food.
Correction: The author emphasizes that the quality of food (halal vs. haram vs. ambiguous) directly affects the quality of one's deeds, so both quantity and quality matter.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the effect of halal food?
A) It can give rise to any kind of deed.
B) It gives rise only to righteous deeds.
C) It gives rise to mixed deeds.
D) It has no effect on deeds.

Key Takeaway
Hunger is a chosen discipline; the quality of food directly impacts the quality of one's deeds.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the last meal you ate. Can you confidently trace its origin to be halal, or is there ambiguity? How might that ambiguity be manifesting in your actions through subtle showing off or self-admiration? Will you commit to investigating the source of your food from this day onward?
?
The author says ambiguous food produces deeds mixed with riya (showing off) and ujb (self-admiration). When you reflect on your daily actions—prayer, charity, speech—do you detect those impurities? If your diet is mixed, what are you willing to change to make it pure halal, even if it means eating less or differently?
?
Fasting is called the 'bridle of travelers.' When was the last time you felt your lower desires actually restrained by fasting? If not, are you willing to begin with frequent fasting to gain that discipline, understanding that hunger is a chosen practice that shapes your deeds?

The Seventh Practice: Seclusion (al-'Uzla)
Dardeer prescribes seclusion except for shaykh, righteous brother, and necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the practice of seclusion (al-'uzla) as prescribed by Imam Dardeer, including its exceptions and purpose.

Core Idea
Seclusion shields the heart; exceptions preserve guidance and necessity.

Imam Dardeer continues his list of soul-purifying practices with the seventh: seclusion (al-'uzla). This practice is not absolute isolation; it comes with clear exceptions. In this passage, he outlines who the seeker may still interact with and why mixing with people is harmful. Let's examine the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage.
Read the Arabic text once without translating. Notice the keywords: عزلة, شيخ مربي, أخ صالح, مخالطة.
The passage ends mid-sentence due to page break.
2
Identify the three exceptions.
The passage permits interaction with three categories of people. List them from the translation.
3
Reflect on the reason for seclusion.
The author gives a reason: mixing earns the heart darkness. Consider why this might be true from a spiritual perspective.
4
Connect to spiritual purification.
Seclusion is the seventh practice, part of a series aimed at purifying the soul. How does this practice support that goal?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع : العزلة عن الناسِ قاطبةً إلا عنْ شَيخه المُرَبّي له، أو أخ صالح يُعينه على العزلة الطاعة والهمة، وإلا لضرورة بيع أو شراء، إذْ مُخالطة الناسِ تُكسِبُ القلب ظُلمة، لو
The seventh: seclusion from all people except from his nurturing shaykh, or a righteous brother who assists him in seclusion, obedience, and resolve, otherwise for necessity of selling or buying, for mixing with people earns the heart darkness, if...
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh practice: seclusion (al-‘uzla). The author states that the seeker should withdraw from all people, except for three categories: (1) his nurturing shaykh, (2) a righteous brother who helps him in maintaining seclusion, obedience, and high resolve, and (3) cases of necessity such as buying and selling. The reason given is that mixing with people causes darkness to enter the heart. The sentence ends abruptly with 'لو' (if), indicating that the explanation continues on the next page which is not included in this window.

Key Terms
عزلة
شيخ مربي
أخ صالح
مخالطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Seclusion means total isolation with no human contact.
Correction: The author permits contact with the shaykh, a righteous brother, and for necessary trade, showing that seclusion is not absolute.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT an exception to the rule of seclusion according to the author?
A) The nurturing shaykh.
B) A righteous brother who helps in seclusion.
C) Any fellow seeker.
D) Necessity for buying or selling.

Key Takeaway
Seclusion from all except the shaykh, a righteous companion, and essential transactions protects the heart from spiritual darkness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Who in your life right now is earning your heart darkness? Are you willing to distance yourself from them, even if it feels uncomfortable?
?
Do you have a nurturing shaykh or a righteous brother who truly assists you in obedience and high resolve? If not, what is preventing you from seeking such a companion?
?
When was the last time you intentionally secluded yourself from people to protect your heart? If you avoid seclusion, what are you afraid of facing in the silence?

The Virtue of Dhikr and Its Quranic Foundations
Describes dhikr as the greatest spiritual pillar with Quranic proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why dhikr is considered the greatest pillar of spiritual practice and how the Quran emphasizes its importance.

Core Idea
Dhikr illuminates the heart and is the key to sainthood.

This passage opens the conclusion on Sufism by establishing dhikr as the foundation of spiritual purification. The author explains that dhikr bestows a brilliant light on the heart, leading to renunciation of worldly attachments, and he supports this with numerous Quranic verses that command and praise constant remembrance.

Guided Path
1
Understand the nature of dhikr
Read the passage to grasp how dhikr affects the heart and why it is considered the greatest pillar.
Notice that dhikr is said to bequeath a brilliant light.
2
Recognize dhikr as the greatest pillar
Identify the claim that dhikr is the greatest of the spiritual pillars and note the reason given: it leads to renunciation of the world.
3
Explore the Quranic evidence
Go through each Quranic verse cited in the passage and understand how it supports the virtue of dhikr.
There are seven verses quoted.
4
Reflect on the connection to sainthood
Consider the statement that perseverance in dhikr is evidence of sainthood (wilaya).
This ties the practice to spiritual rank.

خاتمة في التصوف
القلب، حتى لا يكون فيه سواه، بل جميع الأركان تنشأ عنه؛ لأنه يورِثُ القَلْبَ نورًا ساطعًا، به يزهدُ الدُّنيا التي حُبُّها رأسُ كُلِّ خطيئة، ولذا قالوا: مَنْ أُعطي الذَّكَرَ فَقَدْ أُعطي منشور الولاية، فالمداومة عليه دليل ولاية المشتغل به.
ولكونه أعظم الأركانِ وقَعَ الحتُ عليه في القُرآنِ المجيد أكثَرَ مِنْ غَيره مِنَ الأركان. قال تعالى: ﴿فَاذْكُرُونِي أَذْكُرْكُمْ﴾، وقال تعالى: ﴿الَّذِينَ يَذْكُرُونَ اللَّهَ قِيَاماً وَقُعُوداً وَعَلَى جُنُوبِهِمْ وَيَتَفَكَّرُونَ فِي خَلْقِ السَّمَاوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ﴾ الآية، وقال تعالى: ﴿وَقُلِ اللَّهُ ثُمَّ ذَرْهُمْ فِي خَوْضِهِمْ يَلْعَبُونَ﴾، وقال تعالى: ﴿يَاأَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا إِذَا لَقِيتُمْ فِئَةً فَاثْبُتُوا وَاذْكُرُوا اللَّهَ كَثِيراً لَعَلَّكُمْ تُفْلِحُونَ﴾، وقال تعالى: ﴿وَذَكَرُوا اللَّهَ كَثِيراً وَانتَصَرُواْ مِن بَعْدِ مَا ظُلِمُواْ﴾، وقال تعالى: ﴿وَلَذِكْرُ اللَّهِ أَكْبَرُ﴾، وقال تعالى: ﴿وَالذَّاكِرِينَ اللَّهَ كَثِيراً وَالذَّاكِرَاتِ﴾ إلى غير ذلك.
Conclusion on Sufism: The heart, so that there is no other in it, rather all the pillars arise from it; because it bequeaths to the heart a brilliant light, by which one renounces the world, whose love is the head of every sin. Therefore they said: Whoever is given remembrance (al-dhikr) is given the charter of sainthood (al-wilaya). So perseverance upon it is evidence of the sainthood of the one occupied with it. And because it is the greatest of the pillars, emphasis upon it in the Noble Quran is greater than other pillars. Allah Almighty said: 'Remember Me, I will remember you.' And He said: 'Those who remember Allah standing, sitting, and on their sides and reflect on the creation of the heavens and the earth' the verse. And He said: 'And say: Allah, then leave them playing in their discourse.' And He said: 'O you who believe, when you meet a company, be steadfast and remember Allah much that you may succeed.' And He said: 'And they remembered Allah much and were victorious after being wronged.' And He said: 'And the remembrance of Allah is greater.' And He said: 'And the men who remember Allah much and the women who remember' to other than that.

Commentary
This passage opens the conclusion on Sufism. The author states that dhikr (remembrance of Allah) is the greatest pillar of spiritual practice because it purifies the heart and causes it to renounce the world, which is the root of all sin. He supports this with several Quranic verses emphasizing the importance of dhikr.

Dhikr is the greatest spiritual pillar because it illuminates the heart and leads to renunciation of the world, and its importance is repeatedly affirmed in the Quran.

The Progression of Verbal Dhikr
Classifies dhikr and details the ascent of verbal dhikr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of dhikr and the spiritual progression that verbal dhikr can lead to, even when starting with heedlessness.

Core Idea
Even heedless verbal dhikr can elevate the heart to divine presence.

This passage from Imam Dardir's commentary classifies dhikr into two types and then focuses on the first—verbal dhikr. It explains a remarkable spiritual progression: a beginner who perseveres in tongue remembrance, even while distracted, can eventually attain nearness to Allah where remembrance flows naturally without effort.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice how the author structures the explanation from classification to progression.
2
Identify the two types
Find where the author mentions the two types of dhikr. Note that only the first type is elaborated in this passage.
The second type (heart dhikr) is implied but not discussed here.
3
Trace the progression stages
List the stages mentioned: dhikr with heedlessness → dhikr with presence → dhikr with absence from creation → absorption in oneness. Observe how each stage builds on the previous.
The climax is when dhikr arises without intention, showing complete union.
4
Reflect on the key lesson
Consider why the author encourages perseverance despite heedlessness. What does this teach about spiritual practice?
This counters the misconception that only perfect remembrance is worthwhile.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالذِّكْرُ نَوعانِ الأَوَّلُ : الذِّكْرُ باللسان، وهو شأن أصحاب البدايات، فيجِبُ عليهم موالاةُ الذَّكَرِ باللّسانِ مَعَ تَكَلُّفِ الحضورِ بالقَلْبِ، حتى يصير الحضورُ طبيعةً له.
ولا يتركُ الذَّكرَ لوُجودِ الغفلة فيه، فلرُبَّ ذِكْرِ مَعَ غَفْلَةٍ يرفعه إلى الذِّكْرِ مَعَ الحضورِ، ولرُبَّ ذِكرِ مَعَ الحضورِ، يرفعه إلى الذِّكْرِ مَعَ الغَيبَةِ عما سوى المذكورِ، فإذا غابَ عما سوى المذكور استغرق في عَين بحر الوَحْدةِ، فيصيرُ القلبُ حينئذ بَيتَ الرب تعالى، فينشأ عنه الذَّكَرُ مِنْ غَير قصْدِ ولا تدَبَّر ؛ لامتزاجه برُوحِه وجسمِه.
And remembrance (al-dhikr) is of two types: the first: remembrance with the tongue, and it is the affair of people of beginnings. It is obligatory upon them to persevere in remembrance with the tongue while making an effort to be present with the heart, until presence becomes natural for him. And he should not leave remembrance due to the existence of heedlessness in it, for perhaps a remembrance with heedlessness raises him to remembrance with presence, and perhaps a remembrance with presence raises him to remembrance with absence (al-ghayba) from other than the Remembered. Then when he is absent from other than the Remembered, he becomes immersed in the very sea of Oneness (al-wahda), and the heart then becomes the house of the Lord Almighty, so remembrance arises from him without intention or deliberation, due to its mixing with his spirit and body.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage classifies dhikr into two types and explains the first type (verbal dhikr) in detail. The author describes a progression: the beginner performs verbal dhikr even with heedlessness, which can lead to presence of heart, then to absence from creation, and finally to absorption in divine oneness.

Key Terms
الذكر باللسان
الحضور
الغفلة
الغيبة
الوحدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Verbal dhikr is useless or ineffective if the heart is heedless or distracted.
Correction: The author teaches that even heedless dhikr has value; it can be a ladder to higher states of presence and eventual absorption in divine oneness.

Checkpoint
What is the first type of dhikr according to the author?
A) Dhikr with the heart.
B) Dhikr with the tongue.
C) Silent reflection.
D) Recitation of Quran.

Key Takeaway
Consistent verbal dhikr, even with initial heedlessness, can elevate the soul through stages of presence and absence to the state where dhikr flows spontaneously from the heart.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have likely experienced times when your tongue moved in dhikr while your heart was completely absent. What concrete step will you take this week to persist in that dhikr, trusting that it may be the very ladder to presence—even when it feels empty?
?
The passage describes the remarkable possibility that heedless dhikr can raise a person to presence, and presence to absence. If you truly believed that your current practice—however imperfect—is the seed for such transformation, what would you change about how you approach your daily adhkar starting today?
?
Picture the heart becoming the house of the Lord, with dhikr flowing spontaneously without intention. This is presented as a real outcome of perseverance. Does your current relationship with dhikr reflect a longing to reach that state? If not, what is holding you back from committing to the simple act of consistent verbal remembrance?

Types of Verbal Dhikr and the Best Formula for Beginners
Lists types of verbal dhikr and focuses on the rule for beginners.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various types of verbal dhikr and why 'La ilaha illa Allah' alone is recommended for beginners, along with the rules for its practice in individual and group settings.

Core Idea
The beginner's dhikr is 'La ilaha illa Allah' alone, reserved for the end in group recitation.

This lesson begins by listing the many forms of verbal dhikr, from tasbih to Quran recitation. It then presents the surprising teaching that for the beginner, the fastest-acting dhikr is the single phrase 'La ilaha illa Allah' without the shahada, except at the conclusion of the litany. We will unpack the reasoning and the rules for individual versus group practice.

Guided Path
1
Review the types of dhikr
Read the first sentence identifying the types: tasbih, takbir, Quran recitation, etc. Note that dhikr can be any verbal remembrance.
2
Understand the beginner's best formula
The author states that the fastest in response for the beginner is 'La ilaha illa Allah' alone, without the shahada. The commentary will explain the reasoning.
3
Examine the exception at conclusion
The rule changes at the conclusion (khatm) of the litany. There, one adds the shahada. Also, in some Shadhili paths, it is said every hundred times.
4
Distinguish individual vs. group practice
When alone, one may say it without restriction. In a group, it is only said at the conclusion. This is the practice of the Muhammadan paths.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأنواع الذِّكْرِ اللساني كثيرة، منها: التسبيح والتكبير وتلاوةُ القُرآنِ وغَيرُ ذلك.
وأسرعُها إجابةً للمبتدئ «لا إله إلا الله» مفردةً عنْ مُحَمَّدٌ رسولُ الله على التحقيق فيما عدا الخَتْم، فإذا أرادَ الخَتم ختم بها، وفي بعض الطرق الشاذلية أنه يذكُرُها على رأس كُلِّ مائة، هذا إذا ذكَرَه وحده، أما إذا ذكَرَ معَ جماعة فلا يذكُرُها إلا عِندَ الختم مع إخوانه، ولهذا درج أرباب الطرُقِ المُحمّديّة على الاقتصار عليها.
فإذا كمل السالك فالأفضل له أنْ يضُمَّ معها «مُحمَّدٌ رسولُ اللهِ»، والأفضلُ حينئذ الاشتغالُ بتلاوةِ القُرآنِ؛ ليتَخَلَّقَ به وتُفاضَ عليه العُلومُ اللدُنّيّةُ مِنْ أسراره، فإنْ لَّم يكنْ يحفَظُ القُرآنَ اشْتَغَلَ بسماعِهِ مِمَّنْ يقرؤه وإنْ كان القارئ صاحبَ غَفْلَةٍ، ويكونُ الأمْرُ على حَدِّ قَول العارف بالله تعالى سيدي عُمَرَ بن الفارض رَضِيَ الله عَنْهُ : يا أُختَ سُعْدِ مِنْ حبيبي جئتني * برسالة أديتها بتلَطَّفِ فسمعتُ ما لم تسمعي ونظَرتُ ما * لم تنظُري وعرَفتُ ما لم تعرِفي
And the types of verbal dhikr are many: from them are glorification (tasbih), magnification (takbir), recitation of the Quran, and other than that. The fastest in response for the beginner is 'There is no god but Allah' ('La ilaha illa Allah') alone without 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah' according to verification, except at the conclusion (al-khatm). When he intends the conclusion, he concludes with it. And in some Shadhili paths, he says it at the head of every hundred. This is when he remembers alone. But if he remembers with a group, he only says it at the conclusion with his brothers. For this reason, the people of the Muhammadan paths (al-turuq) have adhered to limiting to it. When the seeker (al-salik) becomes complete, it is better for him to add with it 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah'. And the better at that time is to occupy himself with recitation of the Quran, to become characterized by it and that the ladunni sciences (al-ulum al-ladunniyya) may be bestowed upon him from its secrets. If he does not memorize the Quran, he occupies himself with listening to it from one who recites it, even if the reciter is heedless. And the matter is according to the saying of the knower of Allah, my master Umar ibn al-Farid, may Allah be pleased with him: 'O sister of Sa'd, from my beloved you came to me, with a message you delivered with gentleness. So I heard what you did not hear, and I saw what you did not see, and I knew what you did not know.'
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage discusses the various types of verbal dhikr and recommends 'La ilaha illa Allah' alone for beginners, with the condition of adding the shahada at the conclusion of the litany. It also describes the Shadhili practice of saying it every hundred times individually, and the practice of the Muhammadan paths. For advanced seekers, the author recommends adding 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah' and then occupying with Quran recitation, or listening to it if one does not memorize. He concludes with a poetic saying of Umar ibn al-Farid illustrating spiritual insight.

Key Terms
الذكر اللساني
لا إله إلا الله
الختم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Beginners should always recite the full shahada 'La ilaha illa Allah Muhammad Rasul Allah'.
Correction: The author states that the beginner should say 'La ilaha illa Allah' alone, except at the conclusion of the litany.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does the beginner add 'Muhammad Rasul Allah' to 'La ilaha illa Allah'?
A) Every repetition
B) Only at the conclusion (khatm)
C) When in a group
D) Never

Key Takeaway
For beginners, the most effective dhikr is 'La ilaha illa Allah' alone, with the shahada added only at the conclusion of the litany.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you choose your dhikr based on what is 'fastest in response' for your current state, or do you cling to a formula out of habit or pride? Are you willing to simplify your practice to 'La ilaha illa Allah' alone, trusting the path of the Muhammadan people?
?
If you were in a group dhikr, would you feel comfortable remaining silent while others recite, only speaking at the conclusion? Examine your heart: is your dhikr for Allah alone, or do you seek to be noticed for your devotion?
?
This lesson prescribes a specific discipline for the beginner. Have you been mixing formulas out of spiritual impatience? Will you commit to this focused practice, even if it feels less elaborate, and let the response come from Allah in His time?

Advanced Dhikr: Adding the Shahada and Focusing on Quran Recitation
Advises the advanced seeker to add shahada and prioritize Quran recitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the progression for the advanced seeker: adding the shahada to the dhikr and then prioritizing Quran recitation to attain ladunni sciences.

Core Idea
Once complete, the seeker adds the shahada and turns to the Quran for spiritual unveiling.

Having established the beginner's practice, the author now addresses the advanced seeker. When the seeker reaches a certain level of completion, it becomes better to include the shahada with 'La ilaha illa Allah'. But even more excellent is to occupy oneself with reciting the Quran, as it imparts its secrets and bestows divine sciences. If one cannot recite, listening to a reciter suffices, even if the reciter is heedless. The passage ends with a poetic illustration from Ibn al-Farid.

Guided Path
1
Understand the advanced seeker's practice
When the seeker is complete, it is better to add 'Muhammad Rasul Allah' to the dhikr.
2
Appreciate the superiority of Quran recitation
The author says it is even better to occupy with Quran recitation to become characterized by it and receive ladunni sciences.
3
Learn the alternative if one does not memorize Quran
If one does not memorize, listen to a reciter, even if heedless.
4
Reflect on the poetic illustration
The saying of Umar ibn al-Farid illustrates the spiritual insight gained, as the poet hears, sees, and knows beyond ordinary experience.

?

Heart Dhikr and the Greatest Act: Muraqaba
Defines heart dhikr and proclaims muraqaba as its highest form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that heart dhikr is a higher form of remembrance and that muraqaba (watchfulness) is its greatest expression, requiring truthfulness, struggle against the self, and guidance from a perfect shaykh.

Core Idea
The highest dhikr is heart remembrance, and its peak is muraqaba.

This lesson delves into the second type of dhikr: the remembrance of the heart. While tongue dhikr is for beginners, heart dhikr is the way of those at the final stages. The author singles out muraqaba as the greatest form of heart dhikr, and then outlines the essential components that make this path sound.

Guided Path
1
Understand the distinction between tongue and heart dhikr
The passage defines heart dhikr as a higher level for those at advanced stages. Notice that it contrasts with the previously mentioned tongue dhikr.
Heart dhikr is not just silent repetition but a state of awareness.
2
Reflect on the role of tafakkur
The passage includes reflection (fikr) on the wonders of created things as part of heart dhikr. Consider how contemplation deepens remembrance.
3
Identify muraqaba as the greatest
The author explicitly states that muraqaba (watchfulness) is the greatest form of heart dhikr. This will be elaborated later.
Muraqaba means constant awareness of Allah.
4
Note the indispensable elements
The passage concludes by listing dhikr, truthfulness in striving against the soul, and patience under a perfect shaykh as essential. Understand why each is necessary.
These three form the foundation of the spiritual path.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النَّوعُ الثاني: الذِّكْرُ بالقلب، وهو شأن أرباب النهايات، ومنه الفكر في بدائع المصنوعات، وأعظَمُها المراقبة الآتي بيانها.
وبعضهم يعدُّ الأُصولَ أكْثَرَ مِنْ ذلكَ، وبعضُهم يعدُّها أقل، وفي الحقيقةِ كُلُّها أُمورٌ لا بُدَّ منها، وعُمْدَتها الذِّكْرُ والصَّدْقُ في المجاهدة بمُخالفةِ النَّفْسِ في شهَواتِها، ومُقاساة الصبْرِ على يدِ شَيخ كامل.
The second type: remembrance with the heart, and it is the affair of the people of the final stages. Among it is reflection (al-fikr) on the wonders of created things. And the greatest of them is watchfulness (al-muraqaba), which will be explained later. And some count the principles (al-usul) as more than that, and some count them as fewer. In reality, all of them are matters that are indispensable. And their mainstay is remembrance (al-dhikr) and truthfulness (al-sidq) in striving (al-mujahada) by opposing the soul in its desires, and enduring patience (al-sabr) under the hand of a complete (kamil) spiritual master (shaykh).
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage describes the second type of dhikr, which is remembrance with the heart, for those at the final stages. It includes reflection on creation, and the greatest form is muraqaba. The author notes differences of opinion on the number of principles, but emphasizes that all are necessary, with the core being dhikr, truthfulness in struggle against the soul, and patience under a perfect shaykh.

Key Takeaway
Heart dhikr, especially muraqaba, is the highest form of remembrance, requiring truthfulness, self-struggle, and guidance from a perfect shaykh.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage says the greatest heart dhikr is muraqaba. When was the last time you truly felt the presence of Allah watching over you, not as a thought, but as a reality that changed how you behaved in that moment? Right now, set a 30-second timer and try to be fully conscious that Allah sees you. What happened? Was it easy or difficult to maintain that awareness? This is the first step of muraqaba. Will you commit to doing this for one minute every day this week?
?
You read that the mainstay of the principles is dhikr and truthfulness in striving against your own desires. What is one desire of your nafs that you have been failing to oppose because it is comfortable? Name it. Write it down. Now, the passage says this requires patience under a spiritual guide. Do you have such a guide? If not, what is your plan to find one? If yes, have you been honest with them about this struggle?
?
The dhikr of the heart is for the people of the final stages. You may feel you are far from that. But muraqaba—watchfulness—is the greatest of these heart acts. If you began practicing muraqaba today, aware that Allah sees every move and thought, what is one thing you would change in your life immediately? Will you set a specific time today to reflect on the wonders of creation, as the passage mentions, to cultivate this heart dhikr?

The Seeker Must Avoid All Sins
Explains the condition 'mujtaniban' as avoiding all sins, major and minor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning and significance of the condition 'mujtaniban'—that the spiritual seeker must avoid all sins, both major and minor.

Core Idea
The seeker must avoid all sins, major and minor, to purify the journey.

In this passage, Imam Dardir explains a key condition for the spiritual seeker: being 'mujtaniban'—one who avoids sins. The text clarifies that this avoidance is comprehensive, covering both major and minor sins, and gives concrete examples of apparent sins. Understanding this condition is essential for anyone on the path of spiritual purification.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic phrase and translation
Read the Arabic text and its literal translation carefully. Note that 'mujtaniban' is a grammatical state (hal) describing the seeker.
The term 'hal' indicates a condition of the subject.
2
Identify the scope of avoiding sins
The passage says 'all sins (al-atham) their major and minor.' Recognize that no sin is excluded from this avoidance.
This includes both outward (apparent) and inward sins, though the passage focuses on apparent ones.
3
Review the examples of apparent sins
Look at the examples given: murder, adultery, drinking wine, eating unlawful, backbiting, slander, and looking at unlawful things. These are outward actions that must be avoided.
Apparent sins are those committed by the limbs; hidden sins of the heart are also included, but not explicitly detailed here.
4
Reflect on the implication for the seeker
Ask yourself: Why is avoiding all sins necessary for the seeker? Consider that sins veil the heart and hinder spiritual progress.
This condition is a prerequisite for the journey mentioned in the original text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مجتنبا) حالٌ مِنْ فاعل «خلص» (السائر) أي: لجميع (الآثام) كبائرها وصغائرها، ظاهرِها كالقتْلِ والزِّنا وشُرب الخمْرِ وأَكْلِ الحرامِ والغَيبة والنميمة والنظر إلى مُحرَّم
(Mujtaniban): a state (hal) of the agent of 'khalasa' (the seeker), i.e., avoiding all sins (al-atham), their major and minor, their apparent like murder, adultery, drinking wine, eating unlawful (haram), backbiting (ghiba), slander (namima), and looking at what is unlawful (muharram).
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is a lexical and grammatical explanation of the word 'mujtaniban' which appears in the matn. It clarifies that the seeker must be one who avoids all sins, both major and minor, and gives examples of apparent sins.

Key Terms

الكبائر
الصغائر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think only major sins need to be avoided in the spiritual path.
Correction: The passage explicitly states 'all sins (al-atham) their major and minor,' so minor sins must also be avoided.

Checkpoint

A) Being sinful.
B) Avoiding all sins, both major and minor.
C) Only avoiding major sins.
D) Seeking sins.

Key Takeaway
The spiritual seeker must avoid all sins—both major and minor—to purify the heart and progress on the path.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The seeker must avoid 'all sins'—not just the major ones. Is there a 'small' sin you have been excusing because you think it doesn't affect your spiritual journey? Write it down and decide right now what you will do to stop.
?
Reflect on the outward sins mentioned: have you normalized any of them in your daily life? Be honest with yourself and name one concrete step you will take this week to eliminate it.

The Etiquettes of the Sufi Path
Lists the etiquettes of the Sufi path leading to perfection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key etiquettes (adab) that the Sufi path requires for spiritual perfection, including self-accounting and maintaining discipline.

Core Idea
Spiritual perfection is built on a foundation of disciplined etiquettes.

This passage from Imam Dardir's commentary outlines the essential practices that a seeker must adopt to progress on the Sufi path. These etiquettes range from outward purity to inner humility and constant self-accounting. They form a comprehensive code of conduct that safeguards the heart and nurtures spiritual growth.

Guided Path
1
Contextualize the etiquettes
Recognize that these etiquettes are for the spiritual group (ta'ifa) and lead to perfection.
The phrase 'بها الكمال' indicates their purpose.
2
Read through each etiquette
Go through the list from 'ملازمة الطهارة' to 'محاسبة النفس'. Note the reasoning given for each.
3
Focus on self-accounting
Pay special attention to the last item: muhasaba. Understand what it includes and why it is essential.
Muhasaba covers forbidden, disliked, and excess permissible actions, as well as inner thoughts.
4
Reflect on application
Consider how these etiquettes can be integrated into your daily spiritual practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومِنْ آداب هذه الطائفة التي يحصُلُ بها الكمالُ: مُلازَمةُ الطهارة، والنَّومُ عليها . وعدَمُ كشْفِ العَورة المغَلَّظة في الخلَواتِ؛ حياءً مِنَ اللهِ ومِنَ الملائكة. ومنها : توقيرُ الكبير والشفقة على الصغير والأرامل والمساكين، بل على جميع الخلق. ومنها: الأدب معَ أَهْلِ العِلْم خُصوصًا خَدَمَةَ الشريعة ومشايخ الطريق؛ فإنهم ورثة الأنبياء. ومنها: أن لا يزورَ أَحَدًا مِنَ الصالحين ما دامَ تحتَ التربية قبل الكمال؛ خوفًا مِنْ أن يرى كرامةً أو خُلُقًا في أحَدِهم لم يره في شَيخه، فيعتَقِدُ في شَيخه النقْصَ فيُحرَمُ مدَدَه. ومنها: سوء الظن بنفسه وحُسْنُه بغيره، حتى يرى أنَّ كُلَّ أَحَدٍ أَحسَنُ منه حالا. ومنها: أنْ لا ينتصر لنفْسِه في أمر. ومنها: أن يرى عبادته دائما قد دخلها الخللُ مِنَ الرياءِ والخواطر الردية، ومِثْلُها يستحقُ عليها العقاب لولا مُسامحة الله تعالى له، فيستغفرَ مِنْ عِبادتِه ومِنَ اسْتِغْفارِه. ومنها: أنْ لا يتكَلَّمَ بكلام العارفين مِنَ الفرْقِ والجمع، والبقاء والفناء ما لم يَكْمُلْ، على أنَّ الأولى للكاملِ تَركُ ذلك، إلا لحاجة تقتضي ذلك. ومنها: مُحاسبة النفس على ما ارتكبته مِنَ المُحَرَّماتِ والمكروهات وفضول المباحات، وعلى ما وقع في نفْسِهِ مِنَ الخواطر النفسانيةِ والشَّيطانية، والاستغفار منها. والفرقُ بَينَ الخاطر النفساني والشَّيطاني: أنَّ الأَولَ: يكون بإلحاح على المعصية أو الشهوة، كالطفل الذي يُلح على أمه حتى [؟] ما يُريدُ، فيجب قمعها عن ذلك بمُلازمةِ الذِّكْرِ وبيانِ عاقبة هذا الأمرِ والتَّوجه إلى الشَّيخ والثاني: يكونُ مِنْ غَيْرِ إلحاح، بل يأمُرُ بالمعصية ويُزَيَّنها، فإن طاوَعَه الشخص، وإلا انتقَلَ لأُخرى؛ لأنَّ قصده الغواية على أي حالة تكون، لا معصية بخصوصها.
And among the etiquettes of this group by which perfection is attained: constant performance of ritual purity and sleeping upon it. And not uncovering the severe private parts in solitude, out of modesty before Allah and the angels. And among them: venerating the elder and compassion for the young, widows, and the poor, indeed for all creatures. And among them: good manners with the people of knowledge, especially the servants of the Shari'ah and the shaykhs of the path; for they are the inheritors of the prophets. And among them: that one not visit any of the righteous as long as he is under training before perfection, for fear that he might see a miracle or a trait in one of them that he did not see in his shaykh, and then he would think his shaykh deficient and be deprived of his spiritual support. And among them: thinking ill of oneself and well of others, until he sees that everyone is better than him in state. And among them: that he not defend himself in a matter. And among them: that he sees his worship always has entered into it defect from hypocrisy and base thoughts; and similar to it he deserves punishment for it were it not for the forbearance of Allah Most High, so he seeks forgiveness for his worship and for his seeking forgiveness. And among them: that he not speak the speech of the gnostics concerning separation and union, subsistence and annihilation, until he becomes perfect; rather the better course for the perfect one is to leave that, except for a necessity that requires it. And among them: self-accounting for what he has committed of forbidden things, disliked things, and superfluous permissible things, and for what has occurred in his soul of soul-based and satanic thoughts, and seeking forgiveness from them. And the difference between the soul-based thought and the satanic thought: that the first: it comes with persistence upon sin or desire, like a child who urges his mother until [??] what he wants; so it is obligatory to suppress it by constant remembrance and explaining the consequence of this matter and turning to the shaykh. And the second: it comes without persistence, rather it commands sin and beautifies it; if the person obeys it, otherwise it moves to another; because its intention is misguidance in any state, not a specific sin.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage enumerates the etiquettes (adab) of the Sufi path that lead to spiritual perfection, and concludes with a distinction between two types of inner thoughts. The author lists several key practices: maintaining ritual purity, modesty, respect for elders and scholars, avoiding premature visits to saints, having a low opinion of oneself, not self-advocating, suspecting one's own worship, refraining from advanced spiritual talk, and self-accounting (muhasaba). Within self-accounting, he differentiates between a soul-based thought (khatir nafsani) and a satanic thought (khatir shaytani). The soul-based thought is persistent like a child nagging for what it wants; it must be suppressed with dhikr, reflection on consequences, and turning to the shaykh. The satanic thought is not persistent but beautifies sin; if the person disobeys, it moves to another sin, as its goal is misguidance in any form, not a specific disobedience.

Key Terms
آداب
ملازمة الطهارة
محاسبة النفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT mentioned as an etiquette of the Sufi path?
A) Sleeping in ritual purity
B) Visiting saints regularly
C) Venerating elders
D) Suspecting one's worship

Key Takeaway
The Sufi path requires a comprehensive set of etiquettes that discipline both outward and inner life, culminating in continuous self-accounting.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of the ten etiquettes in this passage do you find most uncomfortable to practice? Why does it make you uncomfortable, and what does that reveal about your ego?
?
The passage teaches that you should think ill of yourself and well of others, considering everyone better than you. When was the last time you genuinely felt that someone else was spiritually superior to you? How would your daily interactions change if you truly believed this?
?
You are commanded not to defend yourself in any matter. Think of a recent conflict where you defended your position. Could you have remained silent and trusted Allah? What would you lose by giving up self-defense?

Distinguishing Nafsani and Shaytani Thoughts
Differentiates between soul-based and satanic thoughts and their remedies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a soul-based (nafsani) thought and a satanic (shaytani) thought based on their characteristics and know the appropriate remedy for each.

Core Idea
Nafsani thoughts persist like a child's demand; shaytani thoughts move on to other sins.

After outlining the etiquettes of the path, Imam Dardir addresses a crucial aspect of self-accounting: recognizing the source of inner thoughts. He gives a vivid analogy to differentiate between thoughts arising from one's own lower self (nafs) and those whispered by Satan. This distinction is essential for effectively combating them.

Guided Path
1
Read the distinction
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the two types of thoughts.
Note the key terms: nafsani and shaytani.
2
Analyze the characteristics
Focus on the characteristic of persistence: nafsani comes with insistence; shaytani does not persist but beautifies sin.
The analogy of a child nagging is for nafsani.
3
Apply the remedies
Understand the remedy for each: for nafsani, use dhikr, reflection, and turning to the shaykh; for shaytani, resistance leads it to move on.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومِنْ آداب هذه الطائفة التي يحصُلُ بها الكمالُ: مُلازَمةُ الطهارة، والنَّومُ عليها . وعدَمُ كشْفِ العَورة المغَلَّظة في الخلَواتِ؛ حياءً مِنَ اللهِ ومِنَ الملائكة. ومنها : توقيرُ الكبير والشفقة على الصغير والأرامل والمساكين، بل على جميع الخلق. ومنها: الأدب معَ أَهْلِ العِلْم خُصوصًا خَدَمَةَ الشريعة ومشايخ الطريق؛ فإنهم ورثة الأنبياء. ومنها: أن لا يزورَ أَحَدًا مِنَ الصالحين ما دامَ تحتَ التربية قبل الكمال؛ خوفًا مِنْ أن يرى كرامةً أو خُلُقًا في أحَدِهم لم يره في شَيخه، فيعتَقِدُ في شَيخه النقْصَ فيُحرَمُ مدَدَه. ومنها: سوء الظن بنفسه وحُسْنُه بغيره، حتى يرى أنَّ كُلَّ أَحَدٍ أَحسَنُ منه حالا. ومنها: أنْ لا ينتصر لنفْسِه في أمر. ومنها: أن يرى عبادته دائما قد دخلها الخللُ مِنَ الرياءِ والخواطر الردية، ومِثْلُها يستحقُ عليها العقاب لولا مُسامحة الله تعالى له، فيستغفرَ مِنْ عِبادتِه ومِنَ اسْتِغْفارِه. ومنها: أنْ لا يتكَلَّمَ بكلام العارفين مِنَ الفرْقِ والجمع، والبقاء والفناء ما لم يَكْمُلْ، على أنَّ الأولى للكاملِ تَركُ ذلك، إلا لحاجة تقتضي ذلك. ومنها: مُحاسبة النفس على ما ارتكبته مِنَ المُحَرَّماتِ والمكروهات وفضول المباحات، وعلى ما وقع في نفْسِهِ مِنَ الخواطر النفسانيةِ والشَّيطانية، والاستغفار منها. والفرقُ بَينَ الخاطر النفساني والشَّيطاني: أنَّ الأَولَ: يكون بإلحاح على المعصية أو الشهوة، كالطفل الذي يُلح على أمه حتى [؟] ما يُريدُ، فيجب قمعها عن ذلك بمُلازمةِ الذِّكْرِ وبيانِ عاقبة هذا الأمرِ والتَّوجه إلى الشَّيخ والثاني: يكونُ مِنْ غَيْرِ إلحاح، بل يأمُرُ بالمعصية ويُزَيَّنها، فإن طاوَعَه الشخص، وإلا انتقَلَ لأُخرى؛ لأنَّ قصده الغواية على أي حالة تكون، لا معصية بخصوصها.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Nafsani and shaytani thoughts are essentially the same, both from Satan.
Correction: The nafsani thought originates from one's lower self and persists; the shaytani thought originates from Satan and is fleeting.

Checkpoint
What is the main difference between a nafsani thought and a shaytani thought?
A) The nafsani thought is from Satan, the shaytani is from the self.
B) The nafsani thought persists like a child's demand, while the shaytani thought moves on if not obeyed.
C) Both are the same.
D) The nafsani thought is about worship, the shaytani about sin.

Key Takeaway
Nafsani thoughts press persistently like a child, while shaytani thoughts are fickle and move on; knowing this helps in applying the correct remedy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a sinful thought that has been recurring in your mind. Have you mistaken it for a shaytani whisper when it might actually be a nafsani demand? What will you do now to suppress it using the remedies from this lesson?
?
When a fleeting sinful thought crosses your mind and then disappears, do you feel relieved, or do you realize it is shaytani and therefore you must stay vigilant? How can you train yourself to recognize such attacks immediately?
?
You have learned that the shaytani thought does not persist—it simply moves to another sin. Have you ever justified a minor sin because it seemed easier to avoid a greater one, unaware that shaytan is simply shifting his target? What commitment can you make today to resist the very first suggestion?

Distinguishing Divine and Angelic Thoughts
Distinguishes divine from angelic thoughts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key difference between divine and angelic thoughts as described by Imam Dardir.

Core Idea
Divine thoughts indicate good without urgency; angelic thoughts urge obedience.

In this passage, Imam Dardir clarifies a subtle spiritual distinction: how to tell whether a thought comes from Allah or from an angel. Both are good, but they differ in how they present the good. This distinction is crucial for spiritual discernment.

Guided Path
1
Read the distinction
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice how the two types are defined.
2
Compare divine and angelic
Identify the two key features: indication vs urging, and the absence of confusion.
The divine thought does not lead to confusion.
3
Reflect on practical application
Consider how you can tell if a thought is from Allah or an angel in your own experience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الفرْقُ بَينَ الخاطرِ الربّاني والخاطرِ الملكي: أَنَّ الأَوَّلَ ما فيه تنبيه على الخير غَير حثٌ، ولا يؤدّي إلى حَيرة. والثاني: ما فيه حثّ على الطاعة. ومنها: مدحُ أعدائه، وعدَمُ التَكَدُّرِ من ذِكْرِهم، والدُّعاء لهم بالمغْفِرَةِ والتَّوفيق. ومنها: الدُّعاء لعُصاةِ المؤمنين كذلك. ومنها : مُطالَعةُ كُتُب القوم؛ ليتعلَّمَ منها الأدب، ويعرف منها حالَ أَهْل الله تعالى، فبالآداب ترتقي إلى مَقامِ الأحبابِ، أَنشَدَنا شَيخنا: ما وهب الله لامْرِئ هِبَةَ * أَحْسَنَ مِنْ عقْلِهِ ومِنْ أَدَبِهْ هما حياة الفتى فإن عدما ، فإن فقد الحياة أجمل به
And as for the difference between the divine thought and the angelic thought: the first is that which contains an indication towards good without urging, and does not lead to confusion. The second is that which contains urging towards obedience. And among them (the etiquettes): praising his enemies, not being upset by their mention, and supplicating for them for forgiveness and success. And among them: supplicating for sinful believers similarly. And among them: studying the books of the people (the Sufis), so that he may learn from them good manners and know from them the state of the people of Allah Most High; for by good manners one ascends to the station of the beloved. Our shaykh recited to us: 'Allah has not given to any person a gift better than his intellect and his good manners. They are the life of the youth; if they are lost, then loss of life is more beautiful.'
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage first distinguishes between two types of spiritual thoughts: the divine thought (khatir rabbani) and the angelic thought (khatir malaki). The divine thought merely points to good without compulsion, and does not cause confusion. The angelic thought actively urges obedience. The author then lists additional etiquettes: praising one's enemies, not being disturbed by mention of them, and praying for their forgiveness and guidance; similarly praying for sinful believers; and studying the books of the Sufi masters to learn adab and understand the state of Allah's saints. Through such adab, one rises to the station of the beloved (ahabba). The author quotes his shaykh's poetic lines emphasizing the supreme value of intellect and adab over life itself.

Key Terms
الخاطر الرباني
الخاطر الملكي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that any good thought from Allah must feel compelling.
Correction: This passage shows that divine thoughts are gentle indications, not forceful urges.

Checkpoint
What distinguishes a divine thought from an angelic thought according to the text?
A) Divine thoughts urge action; angelic thoughts indicate.
B) Divine thoughts indicate without urging and do not lead to confusion; angelic thoughts urge obedience.
C) Both are the same.
D) Angelic thoughts are from Satan.

Key Takeaway
Divine thoughts gently point to good, while angelic thoughts actively urge obedience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel a gentle nudge toward something good but no strong motivation, do you tend to dismiss it as insignificant? How can you start treating such thoughts as divine gifts that deserve attention, even without urgency?
?
Think of a time you felt a strong inner push to obey Allah. Did you attribute it to angels? How does knowing that angelic thoughts urge obedience change how you respond to such urges—do you act on them immediately?
?
Are your inner thoughts more often gentle or urgent? Which type leads you to confusion? Examine your own experiences: do you confuse divine thoughts with personal whims, and how can you distinguish them using the criteria from this lesson?

The Outcome of Striving and Its Sign
Describes the outcome of striving and the sign of perfection in character.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ultimate goal of spiritual struggle: attaining Muhammadan character and the concrete sign that one has achieved it.

Core Idea
The proof of perfect character is indifference to praise and blame.

This passage reveals the breathtaking goal of the spiritual journey: to be clothed with the very character of the Prophet Muhammad ﷺ. But more importantly, it gives a concrete, testable sign that this transformation has taken place — a sign that cuts directly against our natural ego.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to get the overall flow. Notice the shift from struggle to outcome.
2
Identify the two main outcomes
The passage describes a transformation and a resulting status. Find what replaces blameworthy traits and what robes are given.
3
Understand the sign
The passage gives a specific sign of attaining this character. What is it and why is it counterintuitive?
This is the key test of sincerity.
4
Reflect on the significance
Why would preferring withholding and turning away be a sign of perfection? Discuss with the commentary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا جاهَدتَ النفْسَ بما مرَّ هانَ عليها - إنْ شاءَ اللهُ تعالى- الخُلوصُ مِنْ ظُلْمَةِ الأغيار، وتبدَّلت صفاتها المذمومة بالصفاتِ الممدوحةِ، فيخلع الحقُّ تبارك وتعالى عليك خِلَعَ الأخْلاقِ المُحمَّديّةِ مِنَ الحِلْم والعِلم، والشَّفَقة والرأفة والخضوع، والزُّهْدِ والوَرَعِ والسخاء، وغَيرِ ذلكَ مِنْ مَكَارِمِ الأَخْلَاقِ، كَمَا أَثَرْتُ إِلى ذلك بقولي: (لترتقي معالم الكمال) أي: إلى معالم هي الكمالات، وهي الأخْلاقُ المُحمَّديّة، وحينئذ يكونُ هذا العَبدُ خليفةَ اللَّهِ فِي أَرْضِهِ. وعلامة زوالِ الرُّعوناتِ البشَريَّةِ مِنَ القلب، والتَّحَلّي بالأخْلاقِ المَرْضِيّةِ: أَنْ يستوي عِندَه المدح والذم والمنع والإعْطاءُ، وإقْبالُ الناس عليه وإدبارهم، بل يُرَبِّحُ المنع والإِدْبارَ على مُقابلها .
So when you strive against the soul with what has passed, it becomes easy for it — Allah willing — to be purified from the darkness of other-than-Allah, and its blameworthy traits are transformed into praiseworthy traits. Then the Truth, Blessed and Exalted, clothes you with the robes of Muhammadan character: from forbearance, knowledge, compassion, mercy, humility, asceticism, piety, generosity, and other noble traits of character. As I pointed to that with my saying: 'you may ascend to the landmarks of perfection' — that is, to landmarks that are the perfections, and they are the Muhammadan character. And at that time this servant becomes the vicegerent (khalifah) of Allah on His earth. And the sign of the removal of human frivolities from the heart and of being adorned with pleasing character is that praise and blame, withholding and giving, people's turning toward him and away from him become equal in his view; rather, he prefers withholding and turning away over their opposites (giving and turning toward).
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage explains the ultimate outcome of practicing the etiquettes and self-struggle (mujahada) described earlier. When one strives against the soul as previously outlined, it becomes easy to be purified from the darkness of everything other than Allah (al-aghyar). Blameworthy traits are replaced with praiseworthy ones, and Allah clothes the seeker with the robes of Muhammadan character (akhlaaq muhammadiyya) such as forbearance, knowledge, compassion, humility, asceticism, piety, and generosity. The author refers to his statement in the original poem: 'you may ascend to the landmarks of perfection,' explaining that these landmarks are the Muhammadan character itself. At this stage, the servant becomes Allah's vicegerent (khalifah) on earth. The sign of having removed human frivolities (rūʿūnāt bashariyya) and being adorned with pleasing character is that one becomes indifferent to praise or blame, generosity or withholding, and people's acceptance or rejection. In fact, he prefers the latter (withholding and turning away) over the former, indicating complete detachment from worldly ego.

Key Terms
مجاهدة
الأخلاق المحمدية
خليفة الله
الرعونات البشرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think spiritual perfection leads to indifference to everything, including right and wrong.
Correction: The passage specifies indifference to people's reactions (praise/blame, giving/withholding), not to moral obligations.

Checkpoint

A) He performs many miracles.
B) Praise and blame become equal to him, and he prefers withholding and turning away over giving and acceptance.

D) He becomes a political ruler.

Key Takeaway
True spiritual perfection is marked by complete detachment from people's opinions and preferences, preferring even what is disliked by the ego.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Difference Between Divine and Angelic Inspiration
Imam Dardeer defines the two types of inspiration and their characteristics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinguishing characteristics of divine inspiration and angelic inspiration as defined by Imam Dardeer.

Core Idea
Divine inspiration notifies of good quietly; angelic inspiration urges obedience.

Imam Dardeer presents a concise distinction between two types of inner spiritual prompts. Understanding this difference helps in discerning the source of inspirations and guards against confusion. This passage clarifies that divine inspiration is gentle and clarifying, while angelic inspiration comes with a call to action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the difference is framed in two parts.
The text is short but precise.
2
Identify the two categories
Identify the characteristics of divine inspiration (notification without urging, no confusion) and angelic inspiration (urging to obedience).
3
Reflect on practical implication
Consider why this distinction matters for spiritual practice: knowing whether an inspiration is divine or angelic affects how we respond.
Divine inspiration is a gentle hint, while angelic inspiration demands action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الفرق بين الخاطر الرباني والخاطر الملكي: أن الأول ما فيه تنبيه على الخير غير حث، ولا يؤدي إلى حيرة. والثاني: ما فيه حث على الطاعة.
As for the difference between the divine inspiration (al-khāṭir al-rabbānī) and the angelic inspiration (al-khāṭir al-malakī): the first is that it contains a notification of good without urging, and it does not lead to confusion. The second is that it contains an urging to obedience.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage provides a definition distinguishing two types of spiritual inspiration. The author begins by stating the difference. The first, divine inspiration, is characterized by a notification of good without any pressing urge, and it does not cause confusion or perplexity. The second, angelic inspiration, directly includes an urging to perform acts of obedience. This definition is foundational for understanding the sources of internal spiritual prompts.

Key Takeaway

The Fruit of Self-Struggle: Prophetic Ethics and Detachment
Imam Dardeer explains the result of self-struggle: acquiring Muhammadan traits and the sign of spiritual maturity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the outcome of spiritual struggle as described by Imam Dardeer: the acquisition of Muhammadan traits and the key sign of spiritual maturity.

Core Idea
The fruit of self-struggle is becoming Allah's vicegerent through Muhammadan traits, marked by equanimity towards praise and blame.

Imam Dardeer now describes the fruit that comes from the spiritual struggle outlined earlier. It is not merely self-improvement but a profound transformation: the soul's dark traits are replaced with prophetic virtues, and the servant becomes Allah's vicegerent on earth. He then gives a practical sign of this transformation—a remarkable detachment from the reactions of people.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation to get a sense of the author's message.
Notice the structure: first the transformation, then the sign.
2
Identify the two main outcomes
Look for what happens to the person who struggles: emergence from darkness of others, replacement of blameworthy traits with praiseworthy ones, and being bestowed with Muhammadan traits.
The phrase 'فيخلع الحق...' marks the bestowal of noble traits.
3
Understand the sign of spiritual maturity
Focus on the last sentence: the sign is that praise and blame, giving and withholding, and people's turning towards and away become equal; indeed, one prefers blame, withholding, and turning away.
This shows complete detachment from worldly approval.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا جاهدت النفس بما مرَّ هان عليها - إن شاء الله تعالى - الخلوص من ظلمة الأغيار، وتبدلت صفاتها المذمومة بالصفات الممدوحة، فيخلع الحق تبارك وتعالى عليك خلع الأخلاق المحمدية من الحلم والعلم، والشفقة والرأفة والخضوع، والزهد والورع والسخاء، وغير ذلك من مكارم الأخلاق، كما أثرت إلى ذلك بقولي: (لترتقي معالم الكمال) أي: إلى معالم هي الكمالات، وهي الأخلاق المحمدية، وحينئذ يكون هذا العبد خليفة الله في أرضه. وعلامة زوال الرعونات البشرية من القلب، والتحلي بالأخلاق المرضية: أن يستوي عنده المدح والذم والمنع والإعطاء، وإقبال الناس عليه وإدبارهم، بل يرجح الذم والمنع والإدبار على مقابلها.
Then if you struggle against the soul with what has passed, it becomes easy for her—if Allah wills—to emerge from the darkness of others (al-aghyār), and her blameworthy attributes are exchanged for praiseworthy attributes. Then the Truth, Blessed and Exalted, bestows upon you the robes of Muhammadan traits: forbearance, knowledge, compassion, mercy, humility, asceticism, piety, generosity, and other noble traits, as I alluded to in my saying: 'to ascend to the landmarks of perfection,' meaning the landmarks that are perfections, and they are the Muhammadan traits. At that point this servant becomes the vicegerent of Allah on His earth. And the sign of the removal of human vanities from the heart and the adornment with pleasing traits is that praise and blame, withholding and giving, and people's turning towards him and away from him become equal in his view; rather, he gives weight to blame, withholding, and turning away over their opposites.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage describes the outcome of spiritual struggle (mujāhada). It states that when one strives against the soul using the means mentioned earlier, it becomes easy to emerge from the darkness of 'others' (i.e., everything that distracts from Allah). The soul's blameworthy qualities are replaced with praiseworthy ones. Allah then bestows upon the person the 'robes' of prophetic noble traits such as forbearance, knowledge, compassion, humility, asceticism, piety, generosity, and others. The author references his own line in the poem about 'ascending to the landmarks of perfection,' explaining that these are the Muhammadan perfections. Such a person becomes Allah's vicegerent on earth. He then gives a clear sign that human vanities have left the heart: the person becomes indifferent to praise and blame, to receiving or being denied, and to people's acceptance or rejection. In fact, he prefers blame, denial, and rejection over their opposites, indicating complete detachment from worldly approval.

Key Terms
مجاهدة النفس
ظلمة الأغيار
الأخلاق المحمدية
خلع
الرعونات البشرية
استواء المدح والذم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Spiritual maturity means feeling nothing or having no emotional reactions.
Correction: It means not being disturbed by externals while preferring humility; the person still experiences but is not controlled by praise or blame.

Checkpoint
What is the sign that human vanities have left the heart?
A) Feeling joy when praised
B) Preferring praise over blame
C) Being equal in praise and blame, giving and withholding
D) Distinguishing between praise and blame

Key Takeaway
The ultimate fruit of spiritual struggle is to be adorned with prophetic traits and to become indifferent to people's reactions, preferring humility and obscurity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When someone criticizes you for your religious commitment, do you feel hurt or do you see it as a gift that brings you closer to Allah? The passage says the servant prefers blame over praise. What concrete change can you make in your next response to criticism?
?
Think of a recent moment when you craved praise or felt deflated by blame. What does that reveal about the state of your heart? What one step will you take today to detach from people's approval?
?
You are called to become Allah's vicegerent through Muhammadan traits. Which of those traits—forbearance, knowledge, compassion, humility, asceticism—do you lack the most? What specific struggle will you begin this week to acquire it?

The Supplication for Protection from Worldly Distractions
Imam Dardeer explains the supplication and identifies worldly distractions as cut-offs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of the supplication 'Rabbi la taqta'ni 'anka bi qati'' and the importance of approaching Allah with brokenness and humility.

Core Idea
Brokenness before Allah is the key to being protected from that which cuts you off from Him.

Imam Dardeer begins by explaining the line from the poem that implores Allah not to cut the servant off. He emphasizes that this supplication must be uttered with humility and brokenness, for Allah is especially near to those with crushed hearts. The passage then identifies the primary cut-off as anything that distracts the heart from worship, particularly love of wealth, children, status, and worldly desires, supported by Qur'anic verses.

Guided Path
1
Read the supplication and its context
Start by reading the Arabic verse of the supplication and its translation. Notice the instruction to say it with humility (bidhill).
The word 'bidhill' is key—it means with lowliness and submission.
2
Understand the meaning of 'qati''
Identify what the 'cutter' (qati') refers to: any tribulation or distraction that occupies the heart away from worship. The passage gives examples like love of wealth, children, status, and desires.
These are not inherently evil but become cut-offs when they preoccupy the heart.
3
Reflect on the Qur'anic verses
The author cites three verses warning against being diverted by wealth and children. Read them and consider how they reinforce the message.
Verses 64:15, 3:14, and 63:9 are mentioned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقُلْ بدلّ: رب لا تقطعني * عنك بقاطع ولا تحرمني. (وقُلْ) متضرعًا إلى ربك قولًا ملتبسًا (بذل)، فإن الله تعالى عند المنكسرة قلوبهم: يا (رب لا تقطعني عنك بقاطع) من كل فتنة يشتغل القلب بها عن العبودية، من حب المال والولد والجاه والشهوات، إنما أموالكم وأولادكم فتنة، زين للناس حب الشهوات من النساء والبنين الآية، يأيها الذين آمنوا لا تلهكم أموالكم ولا أولادكم عن ذكر الله ومن يفعل ذلك فأولئك هم الخاسرون. ومن القواطع الكبر والحقد والرياء والعجب، ومنها: العبادة لأجل حصول ثواب، أو حصول فتح لدني؛ ليكون من أولياء الله، وإنما شأنهم أن يعبدوا الله تعالى لذاته وامتثالا لأمره ونهيه، ثم إن حصل لهم فتح فذلك من فضله، وإن حجبوا فذلك من عدله؛ إذ ليس للعبد على مولاه حق، وإنما الحق له تعالى على العبد. فالعبد مطلوب بأن يخلص نفسه من الرعونات النفسية، وليس على الله تعالى أن يهبه المعارف القدسية. والذي يعبده لذلك معدود عندهم من عبيد السوء الذين إذا لم يؤجروا يعملوا، وهذا ينافي كونه عبدًا محضًا. قال العارف بالله تعالى ابن عطاء الله السكندري في الحكم: «تشوفك إلى ما بطن فيك من العيوب، خير من تشوفك إلى ما حجب عنك من الغيوب».
And say with humility: O Lord, do not cut me off from You by any cutter, and do not deprive me. (And say) supplicating to your Lord with a word clothed in humility, for Allah Almighty is with those whose hearts are broken: O (Lord, do not cut me off from You by any cutter) from every tribulation that occupies the heart away from worship, from love of wealth, children, status, and desires. 'Your wealth and your children are only a trial' (64:15), 'Adorned for people is the love of desires from women and children' (3:14), 'O you who believe, let not your wealth and your children divert you from the remembrance of Allah; and whoever does that, then those are the losers' (63:9). And among the cut-offs are pride, rancor, hypocrisy, and self-admiration, and among them: worship for the sake of obtaining reward, or obtaining a worldly opening, in order to be among the friends of Allah. But their (the friends') way is to worship Allah for His own sake and in compliance with His command and prohibition. Then if an opening occurs to them, that is from His favor, and if they are veiled, that is from His justice; for the servant has no right upon his Master, but rather the right belongs to Him upon the servant. Thus the servant is required to purify his soul from psychological vanities, and it is not upon Allah to bestow upon him the sacred knowledge. And the one who worships Him for that reason is counted among them as among the evil servants who, if not rewarded, do not work; and this contradicts his being a pure servant. The knower of Allah, Ibn 'Atā'illāh al-Sakandarī, said in his Hikam: 'Your concern for what lies hidden in you of faults is better than your concern for what is veiled from you of the unseen.'
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed commentary on the verse of supplication that asks Allah not to cut the servant off from Him. The author begins by commanding the servant to say the verse with humility, for Allah is with the broken-hearted. He then explains the 'cutter' (qāṭiʿ) as anything that distracts the heart from worship: love of wealth, children, status, and desires, citing several Qur'ānic verses. He lists further cut-offs: pride, rancor, hypocrisy, and self-admiration. A significant point is that even worship done for the sake of reward or for a spiritual opening (fatḥ) to become a saint is considered a cutter, because true saints worship Allah solely for His essence and in obedience to His commands. Any opening they receive is by His favor, and if they are blocked, it is His justice; the servant has no claim on Allah. The servant's duty is to purify himself from psychological vanities, and Allah is not obliged to bestow mystical knowledge. Worshipping for reward makes one like a bad servant who works only for wages, contradicting pure servanthood. The passage concludes with a quote from Ibn ʿAṭāʾillāh preferring concern about one's own inner faults over concern for hidden spiritual secrets.

Key Terms
الذل
الانكسار
القواطع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the supplication ask Allah to protect from?
A) Poverty
B) Being cut off from Allah by any cutter
C) Enemies
D) Illness

Key Takeaway
This supplication is a plea to be protected from anything that distracts the heart from the sole purpose of worship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'do not cut me off from You by any cutter,' are you willing to name the specific love—wealth, children, status, or desire—that has subtly stolen your heart today? What would it cost you to let go of that attachment and beg Allah to protect you from it right now?
?
The author says Allah is with the broken-hearted. When was the last time you felt truly broken before Allah about your distractions, not just intellectually acknowledging them but weeping over how they have diverted you? If you cannot remember, what is holding you back from that brokenness now?
?
You have read that even permissible things like wealth and children can cut you off from Allah. Think of one permissible thing you deeply love. Is your love for it drawing you closer to Allah or pulling your heart away from Him? Make a concrete decision today to realign that love by using it as a means of worship rather than a distraction.

Inner Vices and the Pure Worship of Allah
Imam Dardeer lists inner vices as cut-offs and explains the danger of worship for reward.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that inner vices like pride and hypocrisy are cut-offs from Allah, and that even worship aimed at reward or spiritual openings can be a barrier to true servanthood.

Core Idea
The most dangerous cut-offs are hidden within the soul — pride, hypocrisy, and even worship for reward.

After listing external distractions, Imam Dardeer turns to internal diseases of the heart such as pride, rancor, hypocrisy, and self-admiration. He then makes a profound point: worship done for the sake of reward or to attain saintly status is itself a cutter, because pure servanthood requires worship of Allah for His own sake alone. The lesson concludes with a timeless wisdom from Ibn 'Ata'illah about prioritizing one's inner faults over seeking hidden spiritual secrets.

Guided Path
1
Identify the inner vices listed as qawāṭiʿ
Read the list: pride (kibr), rancor (hiqd), hypocrisy (riya'), and self-admiration ('ujb). Notice that these are internal, not external.
These are often harder to recognize than worldly distractions.
2
Consider why worship for reward is also a cutter
The passage states that worship done for the sake of reward or a spiritual opening (fatḥ) contradicts pure servanthood. Reflect on the idea that the true friends of Allah worship Him for His own essence.
This is a subtle point—even seeking Paradise can become a barrier if it becomes the primary motive.
3
Understand the concept of the pure servant ('abd maḥḍ)
The author describes a pure servant as one who worships without expecting anything in return, recognizing that Allah has no obligation to grant anything.
The servant's duty is self-purification, not demanding spiritual gifts.
4
Apply the wisdom of Ibn 'Ata'illah
Read the final quote: being concerned with your own hidden faults is better than seeking knowledge of the unseen. Consider how this relates to the earlier points.
This shifts focus from outward spirituality to inward purification.

According to the passage, what is the proper intention for worship?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an act of worship, do you ever catch yourself calculating what you will get out of it—peace of mind, acceptance, or even paradise? What would it mean to worship Allah purely for His sake, expecting nothing in return? Are you willing to pray even if you feel you receive nothing?
?
Are you more often concerned with your hidden faults—pride, rancor, hypocrisy in your heart—or with seeking spiritual experiences and uncovering unseen secrets? Which concern dominates your spiritual life? If you were to honestly focus on your inner vices today, what specific change would you make in your next prayer?
?
The passage says the servant has no right upon Allah. Does that truth unsettle you? Do you live as though Allah owes you something—health, success, answered prayers—or do you truly accept that everything is a favour? How would your worship change if you fully internalized that even your ability to worship is a gift, not a right?
